0670022950|5.0|Dr. Steven Pinker's  \\"The Better Angels...\\" provides an absorbing chronicle of violence (the good, the bad, and the ugly) throughout history, documents data that supports his thesis that violence has declined drastically over the past centuries (especially since World War II), and finally provides us with a recipe (what works and what doesn't work) for violence to continue its decline.<br /><br />Chapter 8, 9, and 10 provide us with an absorbing look into the inner workings of the mind that can lead to violent actions and reactions. Dr. Pinker's insightful analysis provide us with the tools to help fight the persistent incidents of violence that plague certain parts of our planet.<br /><br />After dragging the reader (reluctantly for me) through the gruesome history of violence in all its nasty, inhumane forms, Dr. Pinker carefully documents his reasons for the significant decline of violence between and inside countries over the last centuries.<br /><br />The book gives us a chance to step back and view the historical trends in a whole new way, mixing sociology, biology, and history together in a fascinating way. Dr. Pinker provides us with convincing evidence based on a thorough review of relevant studies and historical datasets. He allows readers to make up their own minds about the evidence and theories presented. He explodes many myths and really forces the reader to think and contemplate the significance of what his is trying to demonstrate.<br /><br />I have gained a new insight about many of the anti-violence trends that have occurred during my lifetime. I underestimated the positive impact of the peace-keeping forces, the importance of the UN, and peaceful demonstrations, among others. Also, Dr. Pinker's insights brought new meaning to my rereading of \\"Old Yeller\\" by Fred Gipson. As I start \\"Independence\\" by John Ferling, I understand better the difficult choices made by Great Britain and the American colonies as war approached.<br /><br />Other reviewers have summarized the book well (Eskildson, Nov 7, 2011 and Gintis, Dec 3, 2011). I only wish to emphasize what I consider the most relevant points, theories and perspective that Dr. Pinker presents.<br />The decline in violence can be attributed to:<br /><br />1. The Leviathan - a government monopoly on force to protect its citizens from themselves.<br /><br />2. International Commerce - an exchange of benefits can turn zero-sum warfare (where nobody really wins) into positive-sum mutual profit.<br /><br />3. Feminization - A more feminized world is a more peaceful one.<br /><br />4. Expanding circle of sympathy toward the feelings of others outside the family.<br /><br />5. The Escalator of Reason - Literacy, cosmopolitanism and education. People's reasoning abilities have steadily increased over time which have resulted in more non-violent solutions to conflict.<br /><br />6. The Spread and Evolution of Democracy - democracy by design allows the citizens to resolve their disputes in a non-violent way.<br /><br />I purchased the hard cover version for the details of the graphs. (I wish they would provide a spreadsheet file of the graphs so we could study and manipulate the raw data). The book was slow reading for me since the writing often made me stop to think over each enlightening analysis and theory about the underlying causes of violence and the reasons for its decline.<br /><br />As an American expatriate living in Mexico for the last 30 years, I especially treasured the insights into why outbreaks of intra-country violence continue in many parts of the world. A must read for all of us who wish to understand better the  violence we see every day in the news.011 and Gintis, Dec 3, 2011). I only wish to emphasize what I consider the most relevant points, theories and perspective that Dr. Pinker presents.  The decline in violence can be attributed to:  1. The Leviathan - a government monopoly on force to protect its citizens from themselves.  2. International Commerce - an exchange of benefits can turn zero-sum warfare (where nobody really wins) into positive-sum mutual profit.  3. Feminization - A more feminized world is a more peaceful one.  4. Expanding circle of sympathy toward the feelings of others outside the family.  5. The Escalator of Reason - Literacy, cosmopolitanism and education. People's reasoning abilities have steadily increased over time which have resulted in more non-violent solutions to conflict.  6. The Spread and Evolution of Democracy - democracy by design allows the citizens to resolve their disputes in a non-violent way.  I purchased the hard cover version for the details of the graphs. (I wish they would provide a spreadsheet file of the graphs so we could study and manipulate the raw data). The book was slow reading for me since the writing often made me stop to think over each enlightening analysis and theory about the underlying causes of violence and the reasons for its decline.  As an American expatriate living in Mexico for the last 30 years, I especially treasured the insights into why outbreaks of intra-country violence continue in many parts of the world. A must read for all of us who wish to understand better the  violence we see every day in the news.

I could not put the book down.   I highly recommend this book to everyone and it is very accessible to those who are not finance experts. I was a student that was interested in going to the field of investing before reading this book, but after reading it, I am fairly certain that the world of hedge funds is where I want to be.

0393338827|5.0|This book is either going to interest you or it's not. It chronicles the financial crisis of 2007-2008, explaining the subprime mortgage markets that drove the economy into recession. In my opinion, it's very technical. Most of the vocabulary and some of the concepts will be brand new to the reader. When writing this review, I realized it's hard to talk about the subject because much has to be explained initially. Here's my best shot:<br /><br />A subprime mortgage is one that is less likely to be repaid by the borrower. These subprime mortgages were packaged together into bonds for large investors. For example, an individual subprime mortgage could be rated triple B, but when a group of them were packaged together they were rated triple A (same as a U.S. Treasury bond). Investors wanted more of this safe investment, so more subprime mortgage loans were giving out to less and less deserving borrowers. Borrowers were offered teaser rates: low interest rates for the first 2 years then it would jump up. And little or no documentation was required, credit scores were glanced over, there were no down payments. Basically loans were given to people who had no chance of repaying once the interest rate jumped up.<br /><br />No one really questioned - or understood - what was in these bond packages. How likely were they to default? What if house values stopped increasing? How could a strawberry picker earning $14,000 a year pay off a $725,000 mortgage? Who would even give a loan for that?<br /><br />I've tried to simplify the root of the problem. But truthfully, it's way more complicated. Products were created that held a 100 of these subprime mortgage bonds. Things got so big and out of control way too fast. Companies earned millions of dollars and wanted more. This was all too good to be true and turned into a vicious, self-destroying cycle. You wonder how everyone could be so ignorant and not question what was going on. The few people who dug into the prospectuses and questioned others realized the whole system was going to blow up. They made hundreds of millions of dollars, some even made billions.<br /><br />Out of all the books I've read this year, this was the hardest to read. There is so much new information that I found myself re-reading page after page. And I was reading it very slowly. You have to in order to take it all in. I don't mean any of this as criticism; I think it's important to read the book slowly in order to understand it. The book contains a lot of facts and information. For the most part, Michael Lewis leaves out his opinions until the end. From other stuff I've read or seen, I think The Big Short provides the best explanation of the crisis. I am not saying this is the only thing one should read, but it offers a great starting point to understanding the basics. After reading The Big Short, you ask yourself how did they not see this coming?le system was going to blow up. They made hundreds of millions of dollars, some even made billions. Steve Jobs died at the young age of 56 this past October, and the biography was released a few weeks after.<br /><br />It's best to know a little background on Walter Isaacson. He has written the biographies of Benjamin Franklin and Albert Einstein. I actually read half of the Einstein biography but it was too burdensome for me to finish. Steve Jobs is just as detailed and lengthy (571 pages). Every aspect of his life is covered - work, family, religion, diets, relationships. The smallest details and conversations are included. Besides being one of the first to bring computers to the world, he has revolutionized so many industries: animated movies, music, phones, books, newspapers, retail stores. (Yes, retail stores. Consider this: on average, each of the 326 Apple stores earned $34 million in 2010. The store in New York earned more money per square foot than any store in the world, and it earns more money than any store in New York City - Saks, Bloomingdale's, Macy's, all of them.) His philosophy was people didn't know what they wanted: if Henry Ford asked people what they wanted, they would have said a faster horse, not a car. Often quoted in the book is Wayne Gretzky, who said \\"skate where the puck was going, not where it's been.\\" It's easy to describe him as a genius after reading the book, and history will place him with Thomas Edison and Henry Ford for all the he has contributed.<br /><br />Steve Jobs can apply to many people. I would handle this one with care, and probably not give it to young people or people in recovery.

159102594X|5.0|This book fulfills the usual sword and sorcery cliches in an unusual and interesting way.  We have the battle-hardened Northern barbarian, the powerful magician, the bumbling magician's apprentice, a spoiled young nobleman, and a band of fierce mercenaries.  The author does a great job of transcending the usual fantasy tropes by giving each character a distinct voice and history.  The battle scenes are gritty and realistic, but there is also an undertone of humor to the novel.  Rather than trying to capture it myself, let me give you an example:  \\"A dour-faced woman was working at a spinning wheel on a doorstep, and she frowned at Logen as he walked past with the unconscious apprentice over his shoulder.  Logen smiled back at her.  She was no beauty, that was sure, but it had been a very long time.  The woman ducked into her house and kicked the door shut, leaving the wheel spinning.  Logen sighed.  The old magic was still there.\\"  Maybe I'm actually a battle-hardened barbarian, because this sounds exactly like something I would actually think to myself.  So what should one expect from a good sword and sorcery novel?  Non-stop action?  Check.  Ancient mysteries?  Check.  Palace intrigue?  Check.  Main characters caught up in a conflict beyond their control?  Check.  Moral ambivalence? Not necessarily, but here anyway.  Throw in well-written dialogue, well-imagined characters, an understandable antagonist, and you have the makings of a brilliant series.  The one minor quibble I would have with this novel, and which others have expressed more or less heatedly, is that it serves mostly as set-up without a true conclusion.  In the end all the loose ends have not been neatly tied up.  I am willing to overlook that in hopes that the next installment will work towards that goal.  Absolutely couldn't put it down.  4.7/5 stars.

0765322889|2.0|WARNING: I've only managed to complete around 40% of the book before giving up. Continuing on would have been a hazard to my own health and sanity.<br /><br />First off, there are generally about three major concepts to having a good book, at least in my eyes. I'm no famous author by any means but as a avid fantasy book reader, there are just some things I experience whenever reading a good book no matter who written it. These concepts include:<br /><br />#1. Interesting characters. Any book is not worth reading, fiction or non-fiction wise, if the characters are boring and flat! As the story progresses, you develop certain emotions for certain key characters. Whether you cheer for the protagonist, root for the villain, get teary eyed when a character dies or whatever, interesting and exciting characters is what drives the story forward and compels you to read further on.<br />#2. Awesome storyline. Having an interesting storyline is the second most important aspect to a good book. Obviously if the story fails to capture the readers attention, then the reader themselves will see no point in continuing. While many books seem cliche, I can't put 100% of the blame on the authors because let's face it, what hasn't been done before?!<br />#3. Vivid world and surroundings. The last concept is painting a vivid world for the interesting characters and storyline for the book. With many fantasy books, the authors have pretty much free reign to let their imagination go wild. The hard part is writing and describing it in a way that the readers can share the same visions as the author originally intended.<br /><br />Gardens of the Moon, up to the point I stopped reading, failed on all three concepts. First of all, characters in the book like Paran, Tattersail, Whiskeyjack and Quick Ben are all boring and lifeless. They think the same and even talk in the same fashion as all other characters. The author also did a horrible job at describing the physical traits of the characters! This makes it really hard as a reader to visualize them in our mind. Add the fact that these characters are completely lifeless and what you get are characters that you absolutely have no intention of caring for. You don't care if they die or not because there is no emotional attachment to them! In fact, I don't even know what most of the character's goals are in the story! It seems that they just trudge along for the ride. I sense that vengeance is a big part of it (killing Laseen, the Empress) but the author just doesn't seem capable of driving that point home, hence the confusion.<br /><br />The storyline is one that needs a lot of tweaking. Being that this book is set in a fantasy world, the author must do a good job at explaining things such as the world's history and magic system to the readers. Because this is the first book in the series, it is even that much more important as you are depending on this book to hook the readers in to continue on with the series (in this case, a whopping nine others). Well, the author completely negates this fact and assumes that you already know what has happened in the world. This can lead to a lot of confusion. The book obviously includes a lot of different characters and so there will be a lot of hopping around between them. My main gripe with the author is that he does a pretty poor job at managing them. He jumps around so much that while it may not necessarily be hard to follow along with, it manages to lose the attention of the reader. Only certain authors can do this and get away with it and that is due to their awesome writing abilities. If you've read George Martin, Brandon Sanderson or even Stephen King novels before, you already know what I'm talking about. I hate to have to bring in other authors in a review to make a point but I really have no option here.<br /><br />What small credit I can give to the author is his effort in trying to paint a lush world for this series. However, in the end, it's still pretty lackluster and just can't be compared to other fantasy books I've read in the past. The magic system is a mystery to me and once again, the author doesn't help at all by not going over the details. He might have done this during the later part of the book but its too late for me because I've already given up on this book and series.<br /><br />In the end, while many readers have thoroughly enjoyed this book and series, I along with many others have seen differently. You know you're not nitpicking about things when all of the other negative reviews of this book on Amazon all state the same thing over and over again. Flat and boring characters that you absolutely have no care of whether they live or die, boring storyline that seemingly goes nowhere, lack of history details and explanation by the author, horrible character names (Tattersail?) and a bunch of others. What I'll say here is what I'll say time and time again whenever I leave a book unfinished. Life is too short to invest so much of my time on badly written books. Will it get better as the series progress? I have no idea. I can only hope so for the other readers who chose to continue on with the series even though they hated the first book. If not, I can't even begin to imagine what reading the other nine books will be like.ooks I've read in the past. It was well scripted, well acted, and fun to watch. I was certainly impressed by every minute that passed by.<br /><br />I will say without ruining any surprises, that this is not a show for the faint of heart. I never read other reviews before posting, but I'll bet at least a handful of people disliked this on the sex scenes alone.<br /><br />In the end you'll either love or hate this show. She was the type of character I rooted against, and wanted the utmost bad things to happen to (and her little she-devil friend). B005HV6Y5W|5.0|The Tree of Life, the latest film by Terrence Malick, won the Palme d'Or at the 2011 Cannes Film Festival and has been compared to Stanley Kubrick's 2001: A Space Odyssey. Like that film, it has received polarized reviews from critics and like Malick's previous work it pushes the boundaries of cinema and is not immediately accessible. It's a film that requires you to think, to analyze, and to discuss; it's not a film that you can sit back and watch at any time. In telling the story of one family, Malick takes his film all the way back to the creation of the universe with the Big Bang. This is not a film without ideas and ambition. The Tree of Life does not intend to entertain, but rather to evoke. It's an existential work that's pacing and scope resembles 2001 to such an extent Malick could have titled it \\"2011: An Earth Odyssey.\\"  Lacking a linear plot, there is no description of the plot that I can provide without giving you a false idea of what the film is about. What little I can say is that the film illustrates the childhood memories of Jack (played as an adult by Sean Penn), including his memories of his strict father (Brad Pitt) and his passive mother (Jessica Chastain, whom I find bears a striking resemblance to the Swedish actress Liv Ullman). Throughout the film, as you'd expect if you've seen any of Malick's previous work, several characters deliver whispered voice-overs that ask questions about God, faith, life, death, loss and time.  While the dialogue poses some important questions about the themes explored, it's probably the most irrelevant part of the film as Malick and his cinematographer Emmanuel Lubezski emphasize images over words. Malick is one of the great visual filmmakers and he uses images so beautifully that this film is almost entertaining based on the beauty of the images alone. Whether you love or hate this film you can't deny the beauty of it. Malick's eye, the cinematography, and Jack Fisk's production design make this film a wonder tobehold. There are some incredible shots of nature throughout and even shots that simply feature Penn wandering through a vast wasteland are haunting and startling in their impact.  There are many special effects (yes, including dinosaurs) and even these are fascinating and beautiful, which can also be attributed to Alexandre Desplat's beautiful, operatic score. As many of them have stayed with me long after the credits rolled, I can't rave enough about the images that drive this movie. As a dream-life evocation of childhood, this film succeeds on a remarkably existential, and perhaps even esoteric, level. The final thirty minutes are some of the most beautiful and moving images I've seen on film, but I can't say that I think The Tree of Life is a perfect film nor can I ignore how indulgent much of it is.  It's a film built on images and it's been written that Malick will reject shots without an artistic aesthetic, but these shots do grow repetitious at times. The pacing will trouble many and I admit that the film felt much longer than it was, but that's not to say I was bored by it. While some will be drawn in by the beauty of it, others will be alienated by the slow pacing and the overindulgence. Know this; The Tree of Life is less of a film than an experience and it is best seen on the big screen.  While imperfect, The Tree of Life is a rather profound cinematic tapestry that doesn't spoon-feed its themes to you and instead allows you to interpret them how you wish and come to your own conclusions. It does evoke emotion in such a way that I've already found it growing in stature in my mind and wish to see it again as soon as possible. It begs to be seen more than once as it gives you so much to take in and to consider that a single viewing can't possibly do it justice. It's a pretentious film, but that's not necessarily a bad thing; in fact, it's the best film I've seen so far this year and I highly recommend it for the curious viewer.  GRADE: A-

0439813786|5.0|Not only is this one of the most visually stunning books I've ever come across, but it is an amazing story as well. Partially told in words and partially told via Selznick's beautifully rendered pencil illustrations, this is the story of Hugo Cabret--orphan and clock keeper. Living in the walls of a Parisian train station, Hugo's world is full of secrets and mysteries, including his father's old notebook and a strange mechanical man. When he crosses paths with the curmudgeonly old man who runs a toy booth and a bookish, kind girl, Hugo's world is turned upside down, and he discovers that there are others who have secrets too.  Selznick weaves a complex story and a compelling mystery for readers 9 and up. The characters are vivid, and the setting (Paris in the 1930s) is just the perfect backdrop for the mysteries that unfold over the course of the book. The pencil illustrations and old photographs that pepper the book's pages are simply wonderful--they add such depth to the magic and wonder of the story, which is sure to capture any child's imagination, as it captured mine. Highly recommend this one.

0446691437|5.0|A great book motivates you to take action.<br /><br />The overwhelming reason to read \\"The War of Art\\" is because it will make you write. As I read sections of the book, I became so motivated that I went directly to the computer and started pounding out text for my nest book.<br /><br />Were you put on this earth to be a writer?<br /><br />As Pressfield says, \\"Don't cheat us of your contribution. Give us what you've got.\\"<br /><br />There are three reasons that authors should read Steven Pressfield's book \\"The War of Art\\".<br /><br />1. To Recognize Your Biggest Enemy<br /><br />Your biggest enemy is resistance. The only thing standing between you, and you being the writer that you would like to be, is resistance. If you are a writer who doesn't write, then you know what resistance is. Resistance deforms our spirit and prevents us from utilizing the genius that God gave us, and from achieving what we were meant to achieve.<br /><br />In our brightest and most lucid moments, we can see the person that we were meant to be. What is stopping us form being that person? Resistance.<br /><br />Resistance does everything it can to distract us and discourage us from writing.<br /><br />As Pressfield says, the great secret to writing is:<br /><br />It's not the writing that's hard. What's hard is sitting down to write. What keeps us from sitting down is Resistance.<br /><br />2. To Combat Resistance<br /><br />Pressfield distinguishes between a professional writer and an amateur writer. An amateur writer only writes when he feels inspired. A professional writer writes every day. Resistance hates professionals.<br /><br />A professional knows that she becomes inspired, or taps into her genius, in the process of writing. The Muse doesn't start to come to her aid until she takes the first step.<br /><br />Although the professional makes money from his writing, she writes because she loves to write. The professional is also detached from her work. She knows that the work that she creates is separate from who she is as a person.<br /><br />This allows the professional to avoid flame out and false expectations about the time it takes to write a book (usually twice as long as imagined) and allows her to impartially accept criticism. The actions of others do not define the writer's reality.<br /><br />Regardless of what obstacles or distractions that Resistance may present to us, such as fear, illness, friends, headaches, or rejection, the writer soldiers on.<br /><br />3. To Reach Higher Realms, Beyond Resistance<br /><br />Pressfield points out that there are great forces who become our allies, as we move boldly forward. These forces, like the Greek Gods, take an interest in the affairs of humans, and intercede in our activities. Their activities explain how great works of art have been created.<br /><br />Pressfield identifies the possibility that great works of art, like the Fifth Symphony, may have already existed on a higher plane, before Beethoven ever wrote it. The music only \\"potentially\\" existed, but it didn't have a physical body. It needed a human to bring it into our plane of existence.<br /><br />The Muse may have whispered a few bars into Beethoven's ear. She may have previously whispered the same few bars into many thousands of other ears, but only Beethoven got it.<br /><br />Artists are like mothers. They don't make new life, they only bear it.<br /><br />In the end, writers don't write to advance their own standing. They write for the sake of writing, obey the guidance they get from their Muse, and ultimately, give birth to a work of art.<br /><br />Another great book to inspire authors is [[ASIN:1619490943 Self Reliance]] by Ralph Waldo Emerson.e it takes to write a book (usually twice as long as imagined) and allows her to impartially accept criticism. To Reach Higher Realms, Beyond Resistance  Pressfield points out that there are great forces who become our allies, as we move boldly forward. These forces, like the Greek Gods, take an interest in the affairs of humans, and intercede in our activities. Their activities explain how great works of art have been created. The music only \\"potentially\\" existed, but it didn't have a physical body. It needed a human to bring it into our plane of existence.  The Muse may have whispered a few bars into Beethoven's ear. 1451629184|4.0|I remember when all of this happened. I remember the news broadcasts, the theories, and the hypotheses about what happened to her. And what makes her book so strong is that it explains everything. It was a very smart choice to write the story in present tense. Dugard's details are subtle, obviously that of a child's. and yet through saying so little, she says so much. Ultimately, it would be easy for me to say that Dugard isn't the best writer out there, but I don't believe strong writing or solid diction or things like that are the points here. This isn't a memoir that professors will use to teach students how to write creative nonfiction, but what it does accomplish is universality and humanity. How can anyone not feel for her?   I think the reason why readers will forgive her for things the book may lack is because it is written in her voice. Reading her story is kind of like sitting in a room with her and letting her tell the story to you. She even makes a disclaimer for her rambling, jumbled writing in the introduction, but I didn't think it was necessary. She told a story that many people wanted to hear. She answered questions that many people wanted to have answers for. Perhaps if she had written the story along with a professional writer, it would be easier to criticize her memoir and critique the nuances, but if that was the case, would readers really see her like we do after reading something directly from her? Probably not.  It's not a perfect book, but it's a powerful read. I've studied creative nonfiction for years and one cardinal rule is to keep emotions out of reviews and critiques. Here, I'm making an exception. If anyone ever feels like they're having a bad day, Dugard's story should remind all of us that life could always be a heck of a lot worse and that we should never take what we have for granted.

038541627X|4.0|I'm ashamed to say that this is the first Asimov I've ever read.  Somehow I slipped through life without it until now, but I'm glad I discovered him.<br /><br />Although I didn't like most of the poetry and fantasy, his science fiction is very powerful.  Most of the pieces are rather slow, but engrossing.  All very conceptual.  Not very action-oriented.  The whole of some stories involves men sitting around and talking.  I can't help thinking that my critique group would trash it for that, but I quite enjoyed it.  I would have enjoyed it more if he had cut out some of the mundane physical descriptions of characters.<br /><br />What an imagination!  His tricky issues and fascinating possibilities gave me lots to think about.  Not a book I'll forget it three months, that's for sure. It's amazing how so many of his predictions for the future were so dead right, yet he imagined computers getting bigger and bigger, until Multivac (the most powerful computer) is a mile long and three stories tall.<br /><br />The book is also a product of the times.  Most stories were written in the fifties, and a LOT of them directly or indirectly address issues of WWII, nuclear weapons, nuclear wars, etc.  A great gender inequality also shows itself.  Almost all the main characters are male.  Most of the female characters are silly, doting, 50s housewife typecasts.  Only about three of them have real minds.  Though I found this rather offensive, I also found it an interesting peek into the mindset of the 50s--or at least Asimov's 50s.<br /><br />Some of my favorite stories in the collection, in case you don't have time to read the whole thick volume:  \\"The Feeling of Power,\\" \\"The Dead Past,\\" \\"Living Space,\\" \\"Profession,\\" \\"The Gentle Vultures,\\" \\"All the Troubles in the World,\\" \\"The Ugly Little Boy,\\" \\"Nightfall,\\" and \\"Hostess.\\"

0064407667|4.0|The Bad Beginning is the first of thirteen books that combine to make A Series of Unfortunate Events. Written by Lemony Snicket, this series is commonly praised and adored, yet has its fair share of critics.<br /><br />These books were written in a style that will either work for readers, or it won't. As with any book, a lot relies on the style. For me, I found the style to be intriguing, sometimes witty, and enjoyed Snicket's ways of interpreting words and how the youngest of the Baudelaire's, Sunny, spoke.<br /><br />The series follow the three Baudelaire children, Violet, Klaus and Sunny, as they journey from place to place and are forced into many horrible situations that they must try to escape from.<br /><br />The three children combine what they are best at (inventing, researching, and biting) to run from whatever their third cousin four times removed (or was he their fourth cousin three times removed?) throws at them. That's not to say that he is their only problem, but he is the core of most of the unfortunate events that plague them.<br /><br />Admittedly, there are sections where repetitive explanations dull the impact of the book and push readers out of the story. Still, Snicket did well in drawing readers back in and despite the small imperfections in writing and pacing, it is an enjoyable book and I have no problem recommending it, and the other twelve, to readers looking for a good series that strays from the norm and offers a different view on things than most books do.<br /><br />The characters are charming and readers will find themselves drawn to the three children - each with their own personality. The Baudelaire's are as close and can be and it helps readers feel what they feel, and endure the trials and torment this family is put through.<br /><br />Anyone looking for a unique style and hours of reading pleasure; this series may be for you.

Where I had more trouble was with how the orchestra related to the violin.<br /><br />The first movement begins with the violin accompanied with notes here and there from various instruments, but not obviously related to what the violin is playing. There are several outbursts by the orchestra, as well as long stretches where the violin plays unaccompanied. I almost had the sense of a violinist and an orchestra sharing a stage, but not playing together.<br /><br />The second movement is a bit better, with stretches where a few instruments actually seem to be playing along with the violin. But still there are orchestral outbursts. In the third movement there are places where the whole orchestra seems to be engaged with the violin, but by then it's too little, too late. Unless the solo instrument and the orchestra seem somehow engaged you don't really have a concerto.<br /><br />My thought after my first listening, reinforced by a second listening, was that violin part could form the basis for a nice sonata. But the composer failed to make a concerto out of it.<br /><br />The Tchaikovsky is pleasant and well played, but not reason enough to buy this album.

B005URRCNQ|4.0|The Black Keys may have released THE Rock album of the year with the well oiled machine that is \\"El Camino\\".  The 11 tracks are mostly Rock & Roll raveups, with catchy sing along choruses that stick in your head.  Their last album, the excellent \\"Brothers\\" combined this approach along with soulful ballads and midtempo tracks.  This time around they are aiming for the rafters with a consise and focused album, which is also their most commercial album to date.  But this is not a bad thing, as they have not sacrificed any of the clever lyrics and great melodies that have put them at the forefront of current Rock & Roll.  The soul influence is also firmly intact with the 'Motown meets Spector' Danger Mouse touch on songs like \\"Dead & Gone\\" and \\"Sister\\", and the classic \\"Stop Stop\\".  Songs like these balance the albums anthemic ravers. The Rock & Roll ravers are the real meat and heart of this great album.  \\"Lonely Boy\\", \\"Money Maker\\" and especially \\"Gold On The Ceiling\\" are lighter-raising classics of the highest order.  I mean, how many bands are making Rock & Roll party records like this anymore?  Producer Danger Mouse has managed to keep the grit and edge on this record.  My only complaint (and it's minor) is he murks up Dan Auerbach's vocals on too many tracks.  What he does do brillantly is coax many tones and shades from Dan's guitar. Patrick Carney's drums are at times snappy, then thunderingly loud, but solid as rock throughout.  I think Danger Mouse is looking to create his own Wall Of Sound, and The Keys are the perfect band to realise this vision with.  Hopefully their collaboration will continue.  The vinyl pressing is excellent, and the inclusion of the CD is very nice.  Like their last album, it comes with a cool poster.  Is this 1975?

This book...not so much.  There were so many times when he would stop the flow of the story to \\"prove\\" the validity of his narrative, constantly talking about how \\"truth is stranger than fiction,\\" double explaining how it was possible that he could know certain things or people, and setting up lengthy (and unnecessary) scenes that support minor later details. I couldn't decide if these expositions were King being boldly self-aware or obnoxiously self-aggrandizing (as in \\"OBVIOUSLY I'm the only one who can write fiction that claims to be real but aware that it is fiction!\\"). Several times, he killed my suspended disbelief as well as the reading flow by doing this and that made it worse. I'm willing to give an author some leeway. Not everything needs to be explained to the minutest detail. There came a point where I began skipping through entire sections because I was trying to figure out how they were relevant.   And then, about 550 pages in, it occured to me. JFK's assassination is not the point of the book. The half cracked romance is. It would've been better if he'd titled the book something more pertinent to the theme...like \\"Romance Across Ages...oh, and a president.\\"   Moreover, the book dragged on and on until the last fifty pages where he inserted characters that had no explanation (where the heck did the color coated time-police come from?) and then summed up the changes to the present (made by meddling with the past) as armaggedon. Because that is OBVIOUSLY the ONLY conclusion that we could've jumped to here.   As long as the reader KNOWS going into this book that the plot against JFK is a sub-plot and not the point, they might not feel duped.   As for me, all of the above issues and the twice-a-page references to the \\"harmonic\\", \\"obdurate\\" past made me want to make \\"poundcake\\" out of this book.

B004G25V9S|5.0|As a somewhat older progressive fan I honestly don't know what death metal is, although my understanding is that Opeth, in fact, is a death metal band.  I also understand that this 2011 release is a departure for the group - no more death metal, no more \\"cookie monster\\" vocals.<br /><br />The music alternates passages of bone crushing volume, heavily distorted electric guitar, and a thunderous rhythm section with softer sections of acoustic guitar, acoustic piano, mellotron, and (barely audible) woodwind instruments.  The closing track is an excellent example of the softer style.  Although I like the range in dynamics, I did miss melodic and harmonic development and would have liked a little more variety in meter (most of album is in common time 4/4).  I did like the gloomy feel of the album though.<br /><br />The players are all solid - I like the guitar playing and the acoustic piano playing.  The lead singer has a good voice - a baritone I think.  It is hard to imagine him doing the \\"cookie monster\\" vocal style.  One surprise was to see percussionist Alex Acuna on this record.  The guy played with Weather Report for crying out loud.  How he ended up jamming with a Swedish death metal group is beyond me.  One other interesting thing was to note that the tune Slither was dedicated to Ronnie James Dio - I can hear a lot of those influences on this track, along with Rainbow and 1970-1971 Deep Purple.<br /><br />Mixed by Steve Wilson (Porcupine Tree), the sound quality of the album is very good.  The CD comes with a nice little booklet that features black and white photos of the group in the recording studio - I thought I saw what looks like a real Hammond organ in one of the photos, although I did not see a mellotron.  The album cover art is a bit grisly, although not without its charm.<br /><br />All in all, this is a great album by an interesting group that has changed its style.  Although the music is a bit too far on the hard rock side of the equation for my taste, I can not stop playing it. B005FMZVLQ|5.0|Bold statement in the heading? I do not think so because, for my taste, this is one of the better looking casual watches I have picked up. Let's start with what is so appealing about the 1512 at face-value.<br /><br />The size of the head is over-sized to a point, but not obnoxiously so--it is about 1 3/4\\" across, or about 4 1/2cm. Invicta makes a lot of over-sized heads, but a lot of those come with bulky bezels, or cases. This Force feels like it needs to be over-sized. It does not look odd, in fact; it feels perfectly appropriate. Everything flows together so that it should be displayed at a larger-than-normal size, for they need to be seen. The Force does not draw attention based on any contriving attempts at being ostentatious, but merely because everything as packaged makes for a very fine, and eloquent, looking time piece resting on your wrist.<br /><br />From the band to the crown, everything about this watch is smooth and effortless. The band is one part genuine leather--bottom--and another part cloth--the top--. Though you do not have the flash that a metal band will you give you, the cloth/leather band will give you light weight and freedom of motion that a oyster or jubilee would not. There are always sacrifices. However, the sacrifice here is not as great as going from this band to a common metal, for, even though the band is part cloth, you could wear this with a suit and still be in your rights, and wits, to do so. Seamlessly go from beach/boat watch to the evening's time-teller.<br /><br />I will say, though, that the cloth band is prone to eventually discolor--in this case it would become less black--and become more stiff when exposed to water, or staining. Because this is cloth, though, maybe it could be hand-washed back to good shape.<br /><br />The lugs are interesting. Where most lugs are flush with the case, this one protrudes down and curves. When free-handling the watch you can tell a big difference , as if you have never noticed them before on other watches. It is interesting in a good sense, and seems to curve, and *hold*, onto your wrist more. It does not bring any discomfort, but is unique to this model as comparing to many other common watches. To give you an idea of what it looks like, picture viewing the watch face down dial-side up on a table looking at it from the side. The lugs curve down so that they actually hold up the watch and do no allow it to be flat, or flush on the table. On your wrist, however, they curve around your wrist and the backplate is flush as it should be.<br /><br />There are many details of this watch that are really surprising that I like, and the backplate, of all things, is not an exception. The back is embossed, so that you can really feel the lettering. Also it has a matte, brushed metal, appearance and feel. Because I like things that are great with potential wear, I think the addition of the matte backing is exceptional.<br /><br />As you can see from the look of the dial, you either like it or you do not. Personally, I prefer everything that was chosen about this watch from the color of the second hand to the lettering around the bezel. It all fits together and feels like a light, airy, boating watch. It has a subtly classic look. Though this does have the usual very high Invicta 'MSRP', the current pricing on this equates to a very good return on your money. It is certainly worth it.<br /><br />You know what the face of the watch looks like. You can see the pictures posted covering many of the angles. B002VHI8J0|5.0|Whether David Mitchell's \\"Cloud Atlas\\" is six tales in one, or one tale in six parts, or a bit of both is hard to tell. What is not in doubt is this author's masterful ability to spin a confusing yarn with one hand and then magically unravel the confusion with a well-placed, carefully honed sentence or two at the crucial moment. While each of the individual tales from which this delicious fabric is woven are neither particularly original nor, in some cases, pleasant, there is a richness in their diversity and a splendour in their range which, in combination, is breathtaking. There is a brilliance too to the book's structure, with its climax coming in its centre, so that it becomes a journey of two halves -- inwards and outwards -- rather than following the traditional story arc form with which western readers are more familiar.  There is a beauty too in the interconnectedness and yet separateness of the component tales; meanings and connotations positively coruscate across the mind as the pages turn.<br /><br />All in all, I found this a wonderful read and one from which I simply could not turn away until it was done. Superb!

I understand some of the ones in November, because this case started out at $39.99 and many people thought it was too expensive.  Well, obviously, Amazon listened and dropped the price to $29.99, making this a perfect case for your Kindle Fire!  I did a lot of research regarding what kind of cover I wanted, and looked at all of them.  I knew right away that most of the leather covers/cases would be out of the question, since I did not want any bulk or weight added to my Fire.  So, lightweight, and good protection were my top priority.  However, I also wanted the flexibility of taking the top cover of the case and folding it behind the Fire while I'm using it, since I use my Fire a lot while I'm in bed.  This cover does that perfectly!  In fact, you fold the cover back, slip the elastic band over it so it stays put, and then you can slip your hand under the elastic band, which gives you even more stability while holding it.  Like a previous reviewer said, this case does not cover the screen at all, which I noticed happens a lot with some of the leather cases. Someone else made a comment about the speakers being covered.....huh?  Have no idea what they're talking about, unless it's the fact that when the case is closed and you slip the elastic band over the front, one of the speakers gets covered then, but the only time you use the band is when you're transporting your Fire somewhere, and you want it to be protected.  If you want to listen to it, don't use the elastic band.  Seems pretty simple to me.  Another person said that they couldn't get it to stand correctly, kept falling over.  There again, they must have been trying to do this incorrectly or on some very unstable surface.  I've used the stand and it's perfect for watching a video.    I read Amazon reviews all the time, and I've come to the conclusion that some people are complainers no matter what, or just plain clueless! That's why you need to sift through the reviews and use some common sense over all.  Bottom line, if you want lightweight, no bulk, good protection, fold back cover, good stand, and IMHO a good price, this is the case for you!

B005K0HDGE|5.0|I'll get this out of the way up front: I'm not a Steven King fan, not even a horror fan. What's great about 11/23/63 is that it's not horror, it's as far away from typical King as anything I can think of. It's really an  intricate and detailed combination of a love story with great historical and future fiction. King blends the genres seamlessly.  Jake, who is recently divorced and broken in many ways, is a small town teacher who through a friend, discovers a time portal to the past. The uniqueness of this portal is that the traveler is taken to a specific place and a specific day - always the same place and day. The time traveler can stay in the past as long as desired and when the traveler returns to the present, only 2 minutes have passed. But, if the traveler stays in the past a year, or 10 years, he will age all that time and be that much older in those \\"2 minutes\\" when he returns.  With the JFK assassination as a backdrop, and the time portal that drops the traveler in the US of the past a few years before the Kennedy assassination, Jake travels back to make the future better and avoid the evils he sees that would have been prevented if Lee Harvey Oswald can be stopped. Along the way we get a good background (with some fiction and speculation thrown in) on Oswald's life and the single gunman theory versus various conspiracy theories.  While embedded in the past, we find that Jake falls in love, makes new friends, and becomes part of this new past, while trying to keep 1 step ahead of his new friends who gradually discover he isn't who he says.  In this version of time travel, we find that the past can indeed be changed. And we also learn that at least in King's view, world politics and cause/effect are much more complicated than a simple \\"Save Kennedy Save the World\\" motto would lead you to believe.  So let me come back to say, I'm not a King fan. I bought the Kindle edition of 11/23/63. I like it enough that I'll probably buy the hardcover to keep as well. That's rare for me but this book is worthy of being on a real shelf.

1583334386|5.0|In the introduction, the author asks us to take the approach similar to that of a scientist, a nonjudgmental and curious observer. She does this because she wants to guide us through our real world/real life willpower experiments where some strategies work and others fall flat(and as she notes, failures in lab study are rarely discussed).   The book is organized to follow the author's 10 week Stanford course and provides us with an opportunity to take class each week from the comfort of our home. Every chapter contains opportunities to 1) REFLECT on what we already know and understand about our willpower and 2) PRACTICE an \\"experiment\\" and how it applies in our own lives.   The book is well-researched and the writing is clear and funny. The scientific explanations are accessible and they help me maintain a nonjudgmental position even as I contemplate my own moments of weakness and reflect on my past experiences with temptation and failure. While I haven't had the book very long, I feel as though I have a funny and knowledgeable friend by my side who is gently and humorously awakening me to several \\"aha\\" moments. More importantly I am learning how to find and use motivation to develop my willpower.  This book is for people who want to understand human behavior (including your own) and ways to practice changing this behavior. It is also for people who want to learn basic information or catch up on the most recent brain science as it relates to the psychology of behavior.    I've been anticipating the book's release and I look forward to putting its lessons into practice! I've recommended it to others, including friends and researchers, and I'm happy to recommend it here too.

B003XF1XOQ|3.0|I very much enjoyed the first two books in this series, despite any technical critiques I may have had. The stories were real and the characters oozed humanity, which is why I think it became so difficult to create the conclusion to the story.  Early on, Collins let us know that Katniss's life was never going to be a happy one, and this book continues that trend. We follow the main character on a journey following her experiences in the Hunger Games and we watch as things continuously spiral out of control, yet everything still remains linked to that eternal notion that human beings can be very, very bad and very, very evil.  While that central theme remains a troubling one as you continue to turn the pages, I found that any message I could take away from this book was buried too far under a plot that got a bit out of control and descriptions that were handed to us on a silver platter. Collins, unlike in her other two books in this series, began to hand-feed us explanations and metaphors that really didn't require a guided tour. For instance, a particularly important revelation is made about halfway into the book about the family cat, and his inability to catch the bright spot generated by a flashlight and how this mirrors Katniss's own struggles. Rather than allow the reader to interpret this message, Collins spends several paragraphs using forced, unpolished self-narrative from Katniss to tell us how this cat's struggle is also her struggle. It reads in a manner like she had no faith in us to get this message ourselves. Perhaps we wouldn't have, but it wasn't necessary because Collins never fails to let us know how conflicted Katniss is as a character. We didn't need the cat story to provide that level of detail; it was already quite well established.  In addition, the difficulty of telling the story of a revolution becomes very prominent in this book. Though it was often a bit simplified through teenage emotions and an all-too-present love triangle theme, the book progresses to an extremely riveting final act, in which Katniss is FINALLY forced to truly confront the evil of humanity she has been discovering for 2 and 2/3 books. She has certainly experienced that evil and suffered consequences from it, but never before has she truly had to sit down and make a decision about it. Every action she has taken to this point was a decision generated by very jarring circumstances that would likely make anyone act the way she did. The conclusion of the book offers us a chance to see Katniss taking her destiny into her own hands, finally, and making a true decision in which she has to weigh the costs of taking a human life versus her own need for survival. Perhaps that is the ultimate moment these books have been pushing us to, but it was a bit of struggle to get there through this third novel.  Overall, as I said, I very much enjoyed the first two books, and I think my negative comments are really more based on the idea that the first two novels are considerably better than this one. I recognize the difficulty Collins faced with this novel, in that she had to give us closure on the character of Katniss as well as some sort of closure on this screwed up society, but too much of this book focuses on the completely unimportant love story and the establishment of a rebellion that really isn't all that interesting because all the good moral debates about that very rebellion don't come into focus until the final chapters.  To conclude, I may have some harsh words for this book, but to any readers who would consider reading this: do it! I liked the book, just not as much as the other two. If, like me, you find the first half of the book to be a bit of struggle, please push on, because the conclusion is worth it, and you deserve to know what happens.

B005NQ5LSC|4.0|True story: I was first put on to the whole Ip Man series by an online friend who told me that I absolutely had to see the films because \\"they're like a cross between SCHINDLER'S LIST and ENTER THE DRAGON!\\"  While I found the comparison more than a little off-kilter, I did see the first film, simply titled IP MAN.  It was released in 2008, and it told the story of Yip Man, a grandmaster of Wing Chun martial arts and - as I learned - the man who trained the legendary Bruce Lee.  (So I finally understood the comparison!)  It was a fascinating story - with incomparable fight scenes - set against the Second Sino-Japanese War.  It starred Donnie Yen as Ip Man, and, to date, it spawned one sequel which also starred Yen, but now \\"the legend is born\\" again in IP MAN: THE LEGEND IS BORN with newcomer Dennis To taking the central role as a much younger Ip.<br /><br />(For the record, it's worth noting that the first IP MAN was nominated for 12 awards at the 28th Hong Kong Film Awards, so, yes, it's quite good, fighting and all.)<br /><br />LEGEND revisits Ip Man's story by transporting the viewers back to the man's youth.  This new installment follows the young man as he begins to understand his fighting prowess.  In a town ripe with political corruption, the younger Ip Man sees all that he stands for threatened, so he stands up alone against these local `forces of evil' to protect his temple and his honor.  While this earlier film only hints at the charged political situation between Japan and China that takes center seat in IP MAN and (I'm told) IP MAN 2, I can say with some assurance that you needn't have to see the earlier films to appreciate the story here; there's enough background provided so that LEGEND can be a comfortable `jumping on' point for anyone.<br /><br />However, that's perhaps what I missed between the first IP MAN and this reborn LEGEND.  The first film had a clear villain for the grandmaster's conflict.  When the audience understands the source of the conflict, it's much easier to appreciate how the narrative unfolds.  Ip's \\"adversary\\" isn't as clear in this film, and, at times, I found that a bit of a distraction.  It's a much different story - one without as direct a requirement to showcase the man's fighting philosophy and skills.  That doesn't make it a \\"lesser\\" picture in any measure; it's just that some of the \\"history\\" felt a bit more dramatically contrived than perhaps it was based on the circumstances of Ip's later life.<br /><br />Sure, there's fighting, but one of the fight scenes (a racist Westerner insults Ip Man at a field hockey match) feels inauthentic, though it very well may've happened.  There's a bit of love triange - almost a quadrangle, if you will - but it gets resolved a bit too predictably and formulaically, a sin to many period-based romances, to seem fully legitimate.  When the film's true adversary is hidden - I don't want to reveal much because I think it fairly \\"spoilerish\\", suffice it to say that spies of a certain nature are definitely involved - it's sometimes unclear who Ip Man's real allies are here.  Thankfully, it's still told with much of the same flair as Donnie Yen's earlier contributions, and newcomer To almost seamlessly steps into these younger shoes with similar styling and physical charisma to the central role.  In fact, it's amazing and refreshing to see such physical grace onscreen these days when so much of the filmmaking process has been reduced to post-production special effects.<br /><br />The picture quality is excellent, the choreography is mind-blowingly incredible, and the production detail is superb.  The disc comes with a \\"making of\\" feature that could've benefitted from greater direction, but that's a small quibble.  What's real movie magic here is the story - the exploration of a key figure in the mastery of Wing Chun against the backdrop of history - and the film certainly succeeds on that level.<br /><br />Highly recommended.<br /><br />In the interests of fairness, I'm pleased to disclose that the film folks at FUNimation provided me with a copy of the DVD for the purposes of completing this review.provided me with a copy of the DVD for the purposes of completing this review.

0393064476|3.0|Hmmm...my most honest reaction is to say that there were moments when I wished I'd already finished this book and gone on, but there is a lot of intrigue to this one.  Greenblatt basically poses to offer an example of the way Lucretius' worldview works, through the rediscovery of Lucretius' own epic poem that poses a thoroughly deistic view of things, where the gods care little about our lives and the universe runs and changes on its own devices.  Thus, an out-of-work scribe and passionate book hunter discovers the ancient Roman text in a heap in a monastery, and its resurfacing gives credence to humanist philosophy in a culture where the frightened agenda of the Catholic church and its Inquisition will tolerate nothing but total submission to their view of the universe, and how this manuscript tweaks upon those offering an alternate view and becomes a guide towards promoting that paradigm change.<br /><br />Greenblatt's book does suffer a bit of an overly eager desire to fill in dramatically where documentation leaves some holes, but overall I found his method and argument reasonable and tempered with deep consideration of how a more natural way of thought contested with spiritual demagoguery of its day.  Some of the objections I've read, that Greenblatt is out to make Lucretius the sole progenitor of modern thought, seem to work off little more than some connotations of the title alone.  The focus here is of the effect of THIS book towards actual inquiry into the world rather than forced acceptance of a dogma enforced by torture and execution, not an argument that Lucretius alone brought the world out of superstition.  Greenblatt's passion for the argument may sometimes be a little too prominent on the sleeve and his research and conclusions not always convincing, but the story remains a very interesting one to the end.

B00003CXC7|5.0|Rear Window, directed by Alfred Hitchcock, is well deserving of its #41 spot on the AFI top 100 listing. It is suspenseful and interesting even over 50 years later. It touches on issues we face in our current times. And, perhaps more amazingly, it manages to pass the Bechdel test, something that few AFI movies manage to do! It has two strong, named female characters who talk to each other about a topic other than men. Most movies fail this simple test. In Rear Window, Hitchcock provides equal strength and time to both the females and males in the story line.<br /><br />Stewart is a photographer stuck in a wheelchair, resigned to peering out his windows at his neighbors. In our modern times this might seem a casual thing. Heck, we know the second-by-second details of our far off friends thanks to Twitter and Facebook. However, in the 50s this was considered less of a normal thing to do. He's even scolded by people around him for intruding on their privacy. Amazingly, we really do come to care about these people around him, even though they don't have names. The sad, lonely woman who eats alone and gets roughed up by a guy she brings home. The newlywed couple who goes from bliss to bickering. The loving couple with their cute little dog.<br /><br />Even when Stewart gets concerned about an invalid wife who seems to go missing, he's still being caught up in all the dramas around him. The song-writing guy and his piano. The ballerina with her bevy of admirers. The whole courtyard is fascinating.<br /><br />I love how Kelly shows her strength and surprises him with her independence. I also cringe when her quite astute observations are dismissed as \\"women's intuition\\" - as if they are not based on logic.<br /><br />I do find the ending to be a bit contrived. The killer was an extremely logical, methodical man who planned out every last detail of what he was doing. He knew there were multiple people aware of what was going on. Why in the world would he try to kill just one of them? Surely that would prove beyond a shadow of a doubt his guilt? Why not take off to safety? To me that's the one false note in this story. They needed a more rational reason behind what he does at the end.<br /><br />Still, a great story with a wealth of details. But being a musician, at least a good one that stands out as significant, is more importantly about being creative and not just trying to blow people away with how technically awesome you are. I believe he is still a very immature player who could learn with some more time to make some really good music if he changes his attitude and finds some soul. You become iconic and an artist because you have vision and have something to add to the canon of music/art that hasn't been done or said before. You can't just simply ape it technically better and expect to be thought of as one of the greats. You need to show a depth of the human spirit, and show possibilities of human creativity that haven't been shown before. Playing faster, cleaner, and more technically amazing will not get you there. If you cannot understand this you will simply end up as one of those guys that carves the Taj Mahal into a grain of sand. Pretty amazing and curious, but who cares ten seconds after you have seen it. The production is good. I find no fault in camera angles, edits, sound, etc. I think if you compare this to the average concert DVD, it is better than usual in this respect. A lot of people will think this concert is amazing because, like Joe, they lack the ability to recognize higher order art. They have been brought up in the West where it is mostly about marketing and superlatives, and how fast you play, and where you play, and how much money you make is a testament to your greatness. The concerts is not bad but it's like white bread, all fluff, air, and packaging. I enjoy white bread once in a while too, like I enjoyed this concert, but you have to take it for what it is. I'd enjoy white bread better if it said, \\"Hi, I'm only white bread but let's have some fun,\\" instead of, \\"I'm among the greatest loaves of bread that have ever existed. Historical accuracy??? Where to begin. First of all, at the beginning of each year the Spartan council passed a law that all Spartan men shave their mustaches. In this atrocity almost all men had full beards, including mustaches. The movie accurately describes the Spartans as being unbelievably well trained to hold a line and move as one (protecting the man to the left with shield) and then shows most battle scenes as individual combat. Even at the end (Battle of Plataea) it shows the 10,000 Spartans running pell-mell into battle. No, no, NO! The terror the Spartan army instilled in their enemies was that they came on slowly in perfect step, inexorably, to the accompaniment of flute music. If they ran, it was only the last few steps, to slam into the ranks of the enemy. At Thermopylae, they held the Persians for 2 days+ precisely because they presented them with an unbroken wall of bronze shields bristling with 8 foot spears held OVERHAND (not underhand as in this movie). The Persians didn't have elephants there, nor rhinoceroses, nor were they stupid enough to charge a line of spearmen with cavalry. The Persians were a handsome people, not ugly as depicted here. Xerxes didn't look like a bling-bedecked, hip-hop pimp (like George W he wanted to finish what his daddy started at Marathon); the Persian Immortals didn't wear chrome plated masks nor have hand grenades. I could go on and on. If you like a gore-fest of severed limbs, decapitations and enough blood splattered to please a Dexter, by all means see this garbage!

B00000295A|5.0|Let's face it. The music world of 1993 was very very divided. There were those concerned mainly with matters of media credibility and those interested in the creation of their particular art. On both ends of the pond soul and funk music at this time generally wasn't created. It was being programmed. That mixed with the whole credibility situation wasn't making for much harmony. Than came a British fellow named Jason Kay (known as Jay) and a talented quartet called Jamiroquai. Putting themselves out there in a mix of other groups in the \\"acid jazz\\" genre (often used as short hand for most varities of funk) with people such as Brand New Heavies and Incognito this band didn't exactly have the jam band tendencies of the former,nor the house/dance leanings of the latter. But they did something very special that meant a lot to myself personally.<br /><br />  Equally capable of top notch musicianship and melodic invention,another thing Jamiroquai understood was the value of instrumental production. The music is well mixed,with the right touches of reverb and echo when needed. Basically it's going for a 70's jazz/funk/soul sound that's produced authentically. Jay's vocals,long and very incorrectly compared with Stevie Wonder have a high to mid range lilt about them that are elastic enough to fit these songs. A better musical comparason might be Curtis Mayfield,especially the soul stepping funky soul groove of the opening \\"When You Gonna Learn\\" and it's environmentalist poetry. \\"Too Young To Die\\" extends to a similar groove with a more anti war message. On \\"Hooked Up\\" and \\"Revolution 1993\\" it comes out what fantastic musicians they are well with polyrhythmic jazzier funk grooves with a more instrumental leaning.<br /><br />  \\"Music Of The Mind\\" is similar though on the more mid tempo end of that area. Headhunters style clavinet type stop/start funk is prominant on \\"Whatever It Is,I Just Can't Stop\\" where as \\"Blow Your Mind\\" has this lean sophistifunk sound with Jay's scatting George Benson style with the violin. The band also did for the digeridoo in funk what EWF did for the kalimba: to bring it into the consciousness of the bands sound and hopefully the listener. The title song,with it's disco friendly dance-funk sound reprises the environementalist concerns where \\"If I Like It,I Do It\\" again brings the Mayfield/Impressions type funk/soul to the forefront. Although clearly under the wing of his influence,it was likely too many comparisons that commercialy doomed this band. Jamiroquai stand as very much their own musical animal. Sometimes sweet as funk can be,other times as deep in the groove as you could get. They epitomized everything a 90's era funk band could be. And even for the doubters they have,this album stands very very strong.enson style with the violin. B005MYEQ4U|4.0|Let me begin by stating that I enjoyed this movie. I would encourage anyone who is interested in the arts (literature, painting, etc.) to see Midnight in Paris. It is a lovely romantic drama that provides an escape from our hectic workaday life.<br />The film opens with a montage of scenes of modern-day Paris, with music of the 1920s playing in the background, which seems just a little too long. The scenes are beautiful and the music hearkens back to a simpler, more romantic time, but the montage just went on too long.<br />The juxtaposition between modern day and Paris in the 1920s (Gil's ideal or Golden Age) was too vague. One of the main themes of the movie is Gil's longing for meaning in his writing career and nostalgia for the Golden Age (the good old days, Paris in the 1920s). For me, there was no difference in the feel of the modern day he magically escapes from and the period he is transported back to: just different clothes and technology and architecture. The film would have been more powerful if more of the ills of modern society (which every generation believes did not exist in the \\"good old days\\") were portrayed. The world which the main characters move through seems just as carefree and luxurious as the Golden Age which Gil longs for.<br />There is, however, a nice lesson in the film, learned by the characters, that the good old days were not necessarily so good to those who lived through them and that every generation longs for a simpler, better time.<br />The point at which Gil realizes that all these individuals (whom he believes at first to be modern people playing dress up and acting the part of characters from the Golden Age) are actually the real, living Scott and Zelda Fitzgerald, Ernest Hemingway, etc. should be a defining and poignant moment in the film, yet it passes imperceptibly. One moment we know that Gil thinks he is in modern Paris, and then we realize Gil is convinced the characters are real, yet his demeanor and behavior don't really change. I don't know if this is a weakness in Owen Wilson's acting or the fault of the director/producer, but I never experienced that wonder that a person would experience when they realize,\\"This is not a dream, these are not quirky actors playing a trick on the public, I am really talking to literary and artistic giants who have been dead for many years.\\" I LOVE Owen Wilson, I'm just not sure he was the best choice for this role. His normal expression when the room is on fire is the same one he shows when he realizes he is in the company of F. Scott Fitzgerald and Ernest Hemingway.<br />Allison Pill as Zelda Fitzgerald stole the show. I felt like I was watching the real Zelda Fitzgerald. In contrast, Corey Stoll's performance as Ernest Hemingway was not very good, in my opinion. He  seemed to be a caricature of Hemingway. I felt like I was watching an actor trying to playing Hemingway. Kathy Bates turns in a terrific performance as Gertrude Stein.<br />Having said all that, let me say I did enjoy this movie. I think anyone who is a writer or artist, or enjoys literature and art, will enjoy this movie. I would not laud it as one of the best movies of all time, but I would consider it one of the best movies of the last few years.<br />I recommend (and this is a compliment about the movie) having your laptop at the ready to Google characters and book references if you are not extremely well-read in regards to the authors, artists, and influential figures in the artistic community in Paris in the 1920s (e.g. Gertrude Stein, Jean Cocteau, etc.).<br />I guess in conclusion, I felt like the premise was so good, and there was so much potential in the idea, that I was disappointed in the final product which the producers produced. I had completely forgotten until I was finishing this review that Midnight in Paris was directed by Woody Allen. I'm sure that many people who rave over this movie have fallen into the trap of looking at art subjectively. Whether it is movies orbooks, we often tend to give more credit to the work of an artist who has produced great works in the past than the current individual piece deserves.<br /><br />P.S.I gave this movie 4 stars, but I really wanted to give it 3 1/2 stars. The rating won't allow half stars, and I felt 3 out of 5 would have been insufficient. This is better than an average movie (3 stars), but I'm not sure it is 4 out of 5.more credit to the work of an artist who has produced great works in the past than the current individual piece deserves. B005OK721G|1.0|A handful of French filmmaker Luc Besson's most famous films betray his penchant for femmes fatale, titles like La Femme Nikita, The Fifth Element, The Messenger, and, to an extent, Leon: The Professional. With Colombiana, which Besson wrote and produced, he returns to the genre with the help of director Olivier Megaton, and the result is uneven. Zoe Saldana plays a Colombian woman whose family was murdered by mobsters when she was young. Now, as a grown woman and a ruthless assassin, she sets out on a path for revenge. If the plot sounds familiar, it's probably because the premise is sort of a staple of the revenge thriller, and though Saldana gives the performance her all, there just isn't enough to set Colombiana apart. The movie not only fails to make an impression, but also suffers from erratic and sloppy filmmaking; it'll probably leave you unsatisfied, but if you've liked any of Besson's and Megaton's recent work, you may still find something to enjoy here.

030726999X|2.0|... that I finished all three books in this trilogy.<br /><br />I should preface this review by saying that I'm not usually a reader that will delve more than a chapter or two into a book that does not present as compelling. It's not that I am looking for page-turning sensationalism - in fact, quite the opposite. I sincerely hope to be enamored with the language of a story and I adore dense literature. The sheer quality of the writing usually makes or breaks the experience for me.<br /><br />I started reading Girl With The Dragon Tattoo a while back, and quickly abandoned the effort after 50-60 painful pages. There were some interesting elements, and I love a slow and deliberate mystery, but the abysmal writing turned me off almost instantly. It was a bit like bad progressive jazz... lacking both catchy melodies and intellectual depth.<br /><br />Over the holidays my wife and I saw David' Fincher's adaptation and quite enjoyed it. We both felt it was a few elements short of being a great film, but the characters were enjoyable and the mystery was intriguing, if not a tad on the clich&eacute; side. By far the most enjoyable element of the movie was the relationship between Salander and Blomkvist. After seeing the film, I committed myself to giving the books another shot. A few days later I'd read the remainder of the series, and was none too pleased.<br /><br />Others have undoubtedly mentioned the numerous annoyances in the trilogy, including the (unnecessarily large number of) cookie-cutter characters, superficial relationships, needless sidetracking, poor pacing, abandoned and hackneyed plot points, needless redundancy, cheap dealing of information piecemeal, inconsistent perspective, etc., etc. I also agree with the numerous reviews that point out the fact that these three books could easily have been edited into a single tightly composed story if given time and a proper editor behind it. But all these criticisms aside (numerous though they may be) I think that by far the worst crime is the gross under-use of the truly novel and enjoyable element of the first (and most successful) story... namely, the aforementioned relationship between the central characters. It seemed obvious to this reader that the author was more interested in Salander's revenge and ultimate triumph over those that had wronged her than on the infinitely more interesting personal development she saw in the first story (which ultimately seemed to be undermined and forgotten).<br /><br />All I can say about the political, legal, and conspiratorial elements of the last two books is that they were at best tolerable and at worst sorry affairs. I feel slightly embarrassed for having read all the novels, and wish I'd stuck to my initial instinct about the quality of the first book. This may also be the first (and hopefully last time) that I hope to see Hollywood rewrite original stories for the silver screen.<br /><br />I've read worse... but not many.f the truly novel and enjoyable element of the first (and most successful) story... namely, the aforementioned relationship between the central characters.
